class="mw-body-content">\n\t\t\t\t<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>\t\t\t\t<div id="contentSub"></div>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>\t\t\t\t<a class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Tabloid newspaper from the United Kingdom and Ireland</div>\n<p class="mw-empty-elt">\n\n</p>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the British tabloid newspaper. For the American supermarket tabloid, see <a href="/wiki/Sun_(supermarket_tabloid)" title="Sun (supermarket tabloid)">Sun (supermarket tabloid)</a>. For other newspapers and publications titled Sun or The Sun, see <a href="/wiki/Sun_(newspaper)" title="Sun (newspaper)">Sun (newspaper)</a>.</div>\n<table class="infobox vcard" number</th><td>\n<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.worldcat.org/oclc/723661694">723661694 </a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Website</th><td>\n<span class="url"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.thesun.co.uk/">TheSun.co.uk</a></span></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><i><b>The Sun</b></i> is a <a href="/wiki/Tabloid_(newspaper_format)" title="Tabloid (newspaper format)">tabloid</a> newspaper published in the United Kingdom and Republic of Ireland.  As a <a href="/wiki/Broadsheet" title="Broadsheet">broadsheet</a>, it was founded in 1964 as a successor to the <i><a href="/wiki/Daily_Herald_(UK_newspaper)" title="Daily Herald (UK newspaper)">Daily Herald</a></i>; it became a tabloid in 1969 after it was purchased by its current owners.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> It is published by the <a href="/wiki/News_UK#News_Group_Newspapers_Ltd" title="News UK">News Group Newspapers</a> division of <a href="/wiki/News_UK" title="News UK">News UK</a>, itself a wholly owned subsidiary of <a href="/wiki/Rupert_Murdoch" title="Rupert Murdoch">Rupert Murdoch</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/News_Corp_(company)" class="mw-redirect" title="News Corp (company)">News Corp</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-circ_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-circ-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Since <i>The Sun on Sunday</i> was launched in February 2012, the paper has been a seven-day operation.\n</p><p><i>The Sun</i> previously had the largest <a href="/wiki/Newspaper_circulation" title="Newspaper circulation">circulation</a> of any daily newspaper in the United Kingdom,<sup id="cite_ref-circ_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-circ-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> but was overtaken by rival <i><a href="/wiki/Metro_(British_newspaper)" title="Metro (British newspaper)">Metro</a></i> in March 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In January 2018 it had an average daily circulation of 1.5 million.<sup id="cite_ref-circ_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-circ-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> <i>The Sun</i> has been involved in many controversies in its history, including its coverage of the 1989 <a href="/wiki/Hillsborough_disaster" title="Hillsborough disaster">Hillsborough football stadium disaster</a>. Regional editions of the newspaper for <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">Republic of Ireland</a> are published in Glasgow (<i><b>The Scottish Sun</b></i>), Belfast (<i><b>The Sun</b></i>) and Dublin (<i><b>The Irish Sun</b></i>) respectively.\n</p><p>In 2012, <i><b>The Sun on Sunday</b></i> was launched to replace the closed  <i><a href="/wiki/News_of_the_World" title="News of the World">News of the World</a></i>, employing some of its former journalists.<sup id="cite_ref-GuardRMemail_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GuardRMemail-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> The average circulation for <i>The Sun on Sunday</i> in March 2014 was 1,686,840.<sup id="cite_ref-Sunday_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sunday-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" href="/w/index.php?title=The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Sun_before_Murdoch"><i>The Sun</i> before Murdoch</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: The Sun before Murdoch">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><i>The Sun</i> was first published as a <a href="/wiki/Broadsheet" title="Broadsheet">broadsheet</a> on 15 September 1964,<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> with a logo featuring a glowing orange disc.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> It was launched by owners <a href="/wiki/IPC_Media" class="mw-redirect" title="IPC Media">IPC</a> (International Publishing Corporation) to replace the failing <i><a href="/wiki/Daily_Herald_(UK_newspaper)" title="Daily Herald (UK newspaper)">Daily Herald</a></i>. The paper was intended to add a readership of "social radicals" to the <i>Herald</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;s</span> "political radicals".<sup id="cite_ref-Curran_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Curran-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Supposedly there was "an immense, sophisticated and superior middle class, hitherto undetected and yearning for its own newspaper", wrote <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Shrimsley" title="Bernard Shrimsley">Bernard Shrimsley</a> of Abrams\' work forty-years later. "As delusions go, this was in the El Dorado class".<sup id="cite_ref-Shrimsley2004_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shrimsley2004-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Launched with an advertising budget of \xc2\xa3400,000,<sup id="cite_ref-Sandbrook58_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sandbrook58-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> the brash new paper "burst forth with tremendous energy", according to <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Times_1964,_pp._10-11_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Times_1964,_pp._10-11-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Its initial print run of 3.5&#160;million was attributed to "curiosity" and the "advantage of novelty",<sup id="cite_ref-The_Times_1964,_pp._10-11_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Times_1964,_pp._10-11-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> and had declined to the previous circulation of the <i>Daily Herald</i> (1.2&#160;million)<sup id="cite_ref-Curran_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Curran-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> within a few weeks.\n</p><p>By 1969, according to <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Cudlipp" title="Hugh Cudlipp">Hugh Cudlipp</a>, <i>The Sun</i> was losing about \xc2\xa32m a year<sup id="cite_ref-Grundy_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grundy-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> and had a circulation of 800,000.<sup id="cite_ref-Sandbrook58_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sandbrook58-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> IPC decided to sell to stop the losses, according to Bernard Shrimsley in 2004, out of a fear that the unions would disrupt publication of the <i>Mirror</i> if they did not continue to publish the original <i>Sun</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Shrimsley2004_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shrimsley2004-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bill_Grundy" title="Bill Grundy">Bill Grundy</a> wrote in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Spectator" title="The Spectator">The Spectator</a></i> in July 1969 that although it published "fine writers" in <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Goodman" title="Geoffrey Goodman">Geoffrey Goodman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nancy_Banks-Smith" title="Nancy Banks-Smith">Nancy Banks-Smith</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Akass" title="John Akass">John Akass</a> among others, it had never overcome the negative impact of its launch at which it still resembled the <i>Herald</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Grundy_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grundy-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The pre-Murdoch <i>Sun</i> was "a worthy, boring, leftish, popular broadsheet" in the opinion of Patrick Brogan in 1982.<sup id="cite_ref-Brogan_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brogan-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Book publisher and <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliament of the United Kingdom">Member of Parliament</a> <a href="/wiki/Robert_Maxwell" title="Robert Maxwell">Robert Maxwell</a>, eager to buy a British newspaper, offered to take it off their hands and retain its commitment to the Labour Party, but admitted there would be redundancies, especially among the printers. <a href="/wiki/Rupert_Murdoch" title="Rupert Murdoch">Rupert Murdoch</a>, meanwhile, had bought the <i><a href="/wiki/News_of_the_World" title="News of the World">News of the World</a></i>, a <a href="/wiki/Sensationalist" class="mw-redirect" title="Sensationalist">sensationalist</a> Sunday newspaper, the previous year, but the presses in the basement of his building in London\'s <a href="/wiki/Bouverie_Street" title="Bouverie Street">Bouverie Street</a> were unused six days a week.<sup id="cite_ref-Stokes_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stokes-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Seizing the opportunity to increase his presence on Fleet Street, he made an agreement with the print unions,<sup id="cite_ref-Stokes_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stokes-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> promising fewer redundancies if he acquired the newspaper. He assured IPC that he would publish a "straightforward, honest newspaper" which would continue to support Labour. IPC, under pressure from the unions, rejected Maxwell\'s offer, and Murdoch bought the paper for \xc2\xa3800,000, to be paid in instalments.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenslade,_Ch._9_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenslade,_Ch._9-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> He would later remark: "I am constantly amazed at the ease with which I entered British newspapers".<sup id="cite_ref-ChippindaleCh1_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChippindaleCh1-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <i>Daily Herald</i> had been printed in Manchester since 1930, as was the <i>Sun</i> after its original launch in 1964, but Murdoch stopped publication there in 1969 which put the ageing Bouverie Street presses under extreme pressure as circulation grew.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Murdoch_years">Early Murdoch years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Early Murdoch years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Murdoch found he had such a rapport with <a href="/wiki/Larry_Lamb_(newspaper_editor)" title="Larry Lamb (newspaper editor)">Larry Lamb</a> over lunch that other potential recruits as editor were not interviewed and Lamb was appointed as the first editor of the new <i>Sun</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Telegraph2000_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Telegraph2000-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Lamb wanted Bernard Shrimsley to be his deputy, which Murdoch accepted as Shrimsley had been the second name on his list of preferences.<sup id="cite_ref-Times2016_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Times2016-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Lamb was scathing in his opinion of the <i><a href="/wiki/Daily_Mirror" title="Daily Mirror">Daily Mirror</a></i>, where he had recently been employed as a senior sub-editor, and shared Murdoch\'s view that a paper\'s quality was best measured by its sales, and he regarded the <i>Mirror</i> as overstaffed, and too focused on an ageing readership.<sup id="cite_ref-ChippindaleCh1_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChippindaleCh1-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Godfrey Hodgson of <i>The Sunday Times</i> interviewed Murdoch at this time and expressed a positive view of the rival\'s "Mirrorscope" supplement. "If you think we\'re going to have any of that upmarket shit in our paper," Murdoch replied dropping a sample copy into a bin, "you\'re very much mistaken".<sup id="cite_ref-Brogan_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brogan-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Lamb hastily recruited a staff of about 125 reporters, who were mostly selected for availability rather than their ability.<sup id="cite_ref-ChippindaleCh1_24-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChippindaleCh1-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> This was about a quarter of what the <i>Mirror</i> then employed, and Murdoch had to draft in staff on loan from his Australian papers. Murdoch immediately relaunched <i>The Sun</i> as a <a href="/wiki/Tabloid_(newspaper_format)" title="Tabloid (newspaper format)">tabloid</a>, and ran it as a sister paper to the <i>News of the World</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-ChippindaleCh1_24-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChippindaleCh1-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> <i>The Sun</i> used the same printing presses, and the two papers were managed together at senior executive levels.\n</p><p>The tabloid <i>Sun</i> was first published on 17 November 1969, with a front page headlined "HORSE DOPE SENSATION", an ephemeral "exclusive".<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> An editorial on page 2 announced: "Today\'s <i>Sun</i> is a new newspaper. It has a new shape, new writers, new ideas. But it inherits all that is best from the great traditions of its predecessors. <i>The Sun</i> cares. About the quality of life. About the kind of world we live in. And about people". The first issue had an "exclusive interview" with the Labour Prime Minister, <a href="/wiki/Harold_Wilson" title="Harold Wilson">Harold Wilson</a>, on page 9.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> The paper copied the rival <i>Daily Mirror</i> in several ways. It was the same size and its masthead had the title in white on a red rectangle of the same colour as the <i>Daily Mirror</i>. The <i>Mirror</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;s</span> "Live Letters" was matched by "Livelier Letters".<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Sex was used as an important element in the content and marketing the paper from the start, which Lamb believed was the most important part of his readers\' lives.<sup id="cite_ref-Telegraph2000_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Telegraph2000-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> The first topless <a href="/wiki/Page_3" title="Page 3">Page 3</a> model appeared on 17 November 1970, <a href="/wiki/Stephanie_Marrian" title="Stephanie Marrian">Stephanie Rahn</a>; she was tagged as a "Birthday Suit Girl" to mark the first anniversary of the relaunched <i>Sun</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-C&amp;H_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-C&amp;H-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> A topless Page 3 model gradually became a regular fixture, and with increasingly risqu\xc3\xa9 poses. Both feminists and many cultural conservatives saw the pictures as pornographic and misogynistic. Lamb later expressed some regret at introducing the feature, although denied it was sexist.<sup id="cite_ref-Telegraph2000_25-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Telegraph2000-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> A Conservative council in Sowerby Bridge, Yorkshire, was the first to ban the paper from its public library, shortly after Page 3 began, because of its excessive sexual content.<sup id="cite_ref-C&amp;H47_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-C&amp;H47-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Shrimsley, Lamb\'s deputy, came up with the headline, "The Silly Burghers of Sowerby Bridge" to describe the councillors.<sup id="cite_ref-Times2016_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Times2016-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The decision was reversed after a sustained campaign by the newspaper itself lasting 16 months, and the election of a Labour-led council in 1971.<sup id="cite_ref-C&amp;H47_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-C&amp;H47-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HorrieBBC_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HorrieBBC-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Labour MP <a href="/wiki/Alex_Lyon" title="Alex Lyon">Alex Lyon</a> waved a copy of <i>The Sun</i> in the House of Commons and suggested the paper could be prosecuted for indecency. Sexually related features such as "Do Men Still Want To Marry A Virgin?" and "The Way into a Woman\'s Bed" began to appear. Serialisations of erotic books were frequent; the publication of extracts from <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sensuous_Woman" title="The Sensuous Woman">The Sensuous Woman</a></i>, at a time when copies of the book were being seized by Customs, produced a scandal and a significant amount of free publicity.<sup id="cite_ref-C&amp;H32_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-C&amp;H32-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Politically, <i>The Sun</i> in the early Murdoch years remained nominally Labour-supporting. It advocated a vote for the <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour Party</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Harold_Wilson" title="Harold Wilson">Harold Wilson</a> in the 1970 General Election,<sup id="cite_ref-Thomas_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> with the headline "Why It Must Be Labour",<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> but by February 1974 it was calling for a vote for the Conservative Party led by <a href="/wiki/Edward_Heath" title="Edward Heath">Edward Heath</a> while suggesting that it might support a Labour Party led by <a href="/wiki/James_Callaghan" title="James Callaghan">James Callaghan</a> or <a href="/wiki/Roy_Jenkins" title="Roy Jenkins">Roy Jenkins</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Thomas_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> In the October election an editorial asserted: "ALL our instincts are left rather than right and we would vote for any able politician who would describe himself as a Social Democrat."<sup id="cite_ref-Thomas_35-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_European_Communities_membership_referendum,_1975" title="United Kingdom European Communities membership referendum, 1975">1975 referendum</a> on Britain continuing membership of the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Community" title="European Economic Community">European Economic Community</a>, it advocated a vote to stay in the Common Market.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The editor, Larry Lamb, was originally from a Labour background with a socialist upbringing, while his temporary replacement Bernard Shrimsley (1972\xe2\x80\x9375) was a middle-class uncommitted <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative</a>. An extensive advertising campaign on the <a href="/wiki/ITV_(TV_network)" title="ITV (TV network)">ITV</a> network in this period, voiced by actor <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Timothy" title="Christopher Timothy">Christopher Timothy</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> may have helped <i>The Sun</i> to overtake the <i>Daily Mirror</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;s</span> circulation in 1978.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Despite the industrial relations of the 1970s \xe2\x80\x93 the so-called "<a href="/wiki/Spanish_practices" title="Spanish practices">Spanish practices</a>" of the print unions \xe2\x80\x93 <i>The Sun</i> was very profitable, enabling Murdoch to expand his operations to the <a href="/wiki/List_of_newspapers_in_the_United_States" title="List of newspapers in the United States">United States</a> from 1973.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Thatcher_years">Thatcher years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Thatcher years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Changes">Changes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Changes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In 1979 the paper endorsed <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher</a> in the year\'s <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1979" title="United Kingdom general election, 1979">general election</a>, at the end of a process which had been under way for some time, though <i>The Sun</i> had not initially been enthusiastic for Thatcher. On 3 May 1979, it ran the unequivocal front-page headline, "VOTE TORY THIS TIME".<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Daily_Star_(United_Kingdom)" title="Daily Star (United Kingdom)">Daily Star</a></i> had been launched in 1978 by Express Newspapers, and by 1981 had begun to affect sales of <i>The Sun</i>. So <a href="/wiki/Bingo_(Commonwealth)" class="mw-redirect" title="Bingo (Commonwealth)">bingo</a> was introduced as a marketing tool and a 2p drop in cover price removed the <i>Daily Star</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s competitive advantage opening a new circulation battle which resulted in <i>The Sun</i> neutralising the threat of the new paper.<sup id="cite_ref-Page331_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Page331-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> The new editor of <i>The Sun</i>, <a href="/wiki/Kelvin_MacKenzie" title="Kelvin MacKenzie">Kelvin MacKenzie</a>, took up his post in 1981 just after these developments,<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> and "changed the British tabloid concept more profoundly than [Larry] Lamb did", according to Bruce Page; under MacKenzie<sup id="cite_ref-Page331_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Page331-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> the paper became "more outrageous, opinionated and irreverent than anything ever produced in Britain".<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Falklands_War">The Falklands War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: The Falklands War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Sun_(Gotcha).png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/58/The_Sun_%28Gotcha%29.png/170px-The_Sun_%28Gotcha%29.png" width="170" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/58/The_Sun_%28Gotcha%29.png/255px-The_Sun_%28Gotcha%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/5/58/The_Sun_%28Gotcha%29.png 2x" data-file-width="278" data-file-height="360" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Sun_(Gotcha).png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Front page of <i>The Sun</i> (4 May 1982) in early editions following the torpedoing of the <i><a href="/wiki/ARA_General_Belgrano" title="ARA General Belgrano">Belgrano</a>.</i> This headline was published before it was known the sinking of the vessel had cost 368 lives.<sup id="cite_ref-Page333_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Page333-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p><i>The Sun</i> became an ardent supporter of the <a href="/wiki/Falklands_War" title="Falklands War">Falklands War</a>. The coverage "captured the zeitgeist", according to <a href="/wiki/Roy_Greenslade" title="Roy Greenslade">Roy Greenslade</a>, assistant editor at the time (though privately an opponent of the war), but was also "xenophobic, bloody-minded, ruthless, often reckless, black-humoured and ultimately triumphalist."<sup id="cite_ref-Greenslade2002_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenslade2002-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 1 May, <i>The Sun</i> claimed to have "<a href="/wiki/Sponsor_(commercial)" title="Sponsor (commercial)">sponsored</a>" a British missile. Under the headline "Stick This Up Your Junta: A Sun missile for Galtieri\xe2\x80\x99s gauchos",<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> the newspaper published a photograph of a missile (actually a <a href="/wiki/UGM-27_Polaris" title="UGM-27 Polaris">Polaris missile</a> stock shot from the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Defence_(United_Kingdom)" title="Ministry of Defence (United Kingdom)">Ministry of Defence</a>) which had a large <i>Sun</i> logo printed on its side with the caption "Here It Comes, Senors..." underneath.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenslade2002_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenslade2002-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Chippindale138_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chippindale138-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> The paper explained that it was "sponsoring" the missile by contributing to the eventual victory party on <a href="/wiki/HMS_Invincible_(R05)" title="HMS Invincible (R05)">HMS&#160;<i>Invincible</i></a> when the war ended. In copy written by <a href="/wiki/Wendy_Henry" title="Wendy Henry">Wendy Henry</a>, the paper said that the missile would shortly be used against Argentinian forces. Tony Snow, <i>The Sun</i> journalist on <i>Invincible</i> who had "signed" the missile, reported a few days later that it had hit an Argentinian target.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenslade2002_45-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenslade2002-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Chippindale138_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chippindale138-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>One of the paper\'s best known front pages, published on 4 May 1982, commemorated the torpedoing of the Argentine ship the <i><a href="/wiki/ARA_General_Belgrano" title="ARA General Belgrano">General Belgrano</a></i> by running the story under the headline "GOTCHA".<sup id="cite_ref-BJR_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BJR-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> At MacKenzie\'s insistence, and against the wishes of Murdoch (the mogul was present because almost all the journalists were on strike),<sup id="cite_ref-Greenslade445_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenslade445-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> the headline was changed for later editions after the extent of Argentinian casualties became known.<sup id="cite_ref-Page333_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Page333-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ChippindaleCh7_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChippindaleCh7-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> John Shirley, a reporter for <i>The Sunday Times</i>, witnessed copies of this edition of <i>The Sun</i> being thrown overboard by sailors and marines on <a href="/wiki/HMS_Fearless_(L10)" title="HMS Fearless (L10)">HMS&#160;<i>Fearless</i></a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenslade445_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenslade445-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After <a href="/wiki/HMS_Sheffield_(D80)" title="HMS Sheffield (D80)">HMS&#160;<i>Sheffield</i></a> was wrecked by an Argentinian attack, <i>The Sun</i> was heavily criticised and even mocked for its coverage of the war in <i>The Daily Mirror</i> and <i>The Guardian</i>, and the wider media queried the veracity of official information and worried about the number of casualties, <i>The Sun</i> gave its response. "There are traitors in our midst", wrote leader writer Ronald Spark on 7 May, accusing commentators on <i>Daily Mirror</i> and <i>The Guardian</i>, plus the BBC\'s defence correspondent <a href="/wiki/Peter_Snow" title="Peter Snow">Peter Snow</a>, of "treason" for aspects of their coverage.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Satirical" class="mw-redirect" title="Satirical">satirical</a> magazine <i><a href="/wiki/Private_Eye_(magazine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Private Eye (magazine)">Private Eye</a></i> mocked and lampooned what they regarded as the paper\'s <a href="/wiki/Jingoistic" class="mw-redirect" title="Jingoistic">jingoistic</a> coverage, most memorably with the mock-<i>Sun</i> headline "KILL AN ARGIE, WIN A <a href="/wiki/Rover_Metro" class="mw-redirect" title="Rover Metro">METRO</a>!", to which MacKenzie is said to have jokingly responded, "Why didn\'t we think of that?"<sup id="cite_ref-Greenslade2002_45-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenslade2002-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Sun_and_the_Labour_Party"><i>The Sun</i> and the Labour Party</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: The Sun and the Labour Party">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>These years included what was called "spectacularly malicious coverage"<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> of the Labour Party by <i>The Sun</i> and other newspapers. During the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1983" title="United Kingdom general election, 1983">general election of 1983</a> <i>The Sun</i> ran a front page featuring an unflattering photograph of <a href="/wiki/Michael_Foot" title="Michael Foot">Michael Foot</a>, then aged almost 70, claiming he was unfit to be Prime Minister on grounds of his age, appearance and policies, alongside the headline "Do You Really Want This Old Fool To Run Britain?"<sup id="cite_ref-StillShines_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StillShines-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> A year later, in 1984, <i>The Sun</i> made clear its enthusiastic support for the re-election of <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a> as president in the USA. Reagan was two weeks off his 74th birthday when he started his second term, in January 1985.\n</p><p>On 1 March 1984 the newspaper extensively quoted a respected American psychiatrist claiming that British left-wing politician <a href="/wiki/Tony_Benn" title="Tony Benn">Tony Benn</a> was "insane", with the psychiatrist discussing various aspects of Benn\'s supposed pathology.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> The story, which appeared on the day of the <a href="/wiki/Chesterfield_by-election,_1984" title="Chesterfield by-election, 1984">Chesterfield byelection</a> in which Benn was standing, was discredited when the psychiatrist quoted by <i>The Sun</i> publicly denounced the article and described the false quotes attributed to him as "absurd", <i>The Sun</i> having apparently fabricated the entire piece. The newspaper made frequent scathing attacks on what the paper called the "<a href="/wiki/Loony_left" title="Loony left">loony left</a>" element within the Labour Party<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> and on institutions supposedly controlled by it. <a href="/wiki/Ken_Livingstone" title="Ken Livingstone">Ken Livingstone</a>, the leader of the left-wing <a href="/wiki/Greater_London_Council" title="Greater London Council">Greater London Council</a>, was described as "the most odious man in Britain"<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> in October 1981.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>The Sun</i>, during the <a href="/wiki/UK_miners%27_strike_(1984%E2%80%931985)" class="mw-redirect" title="UK miners&#39; strike (1984\xe2\x80\x931985)">miners\' strike of 1984\xe2\x80\x9385</a>, supported the police and the Thatcher government against the striking <a href="/wiki/National_Union_of_Mineworkers_(Great_Britain)" title="National Union of Mineworkers (Great Britain)">NUM</a> miners, and in particular the union\'s president, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Scargill" title="Arthur Scargill">Arthur Scargill</a>. On 23 May 1984, <i>The Sun</i> prepared a front page with the headline "Mine <a href="/wiki/F%C3%BChrer" title="F\xc3\xbchrer">F\xc3\xbchrer</a>" and a photograph of Scargill with his arm in the air, a pose which made him look as though he was giving a <a href="/wiki/Nazi_salute" title="Nazi salute">Nazi salute</a>. The print workers at <i>The Sun</i> refused to print it.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> <i>The Sun</i> strongly supported the April 1986 bombing of <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a> by the US, which was launched from British bases. Several civilians were killed during the bombing. Their leader was "Right Ron, Right Maggie".<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> That year, Labour MP <a href="/wiki/Clare_Short" title="Clare Short">Clare Short</a> attempted in vain to persuade Parliament to outlaw the pictures on Page Three and gained opprobrium from the newspaper for her stand.\n</p><p>During the 1987 general election, <i>The Sun</i> ran a mock-editorial entitled "Why I\'m Backing <a href="/wiki/Neil_Kinnock" title="Neil Kinnock">Kinnock</a>, by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stalin" title="Joseph Stalin">Stalin</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Murdoch.27s_response"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Murdoch\'s_response">Murdoch\'s response</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Murdoch&#039;s response">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Murdoch has responded to some of the arguments against the newspaper by saying that critics are "snobs" who want to "impose their tastes on everyone else", while MacKenzie claims the same critics are people who, if they ever had a "popular idea", would have to "go and lie down in a dark room for half an hour". Both have pointed to the huge commercial success of the <i>Sun</i> in this period and its establishment as Britain\'s top-selling newspaper, claiming that they are "giving the public what they want". This conclusion is disputed by critics. <a href="/wiki/John_Pilger" title="John Pilger">John Pilger</a> has said that a late-1970s edition of the <i>Daily Mirror</i>, which replaced the usual celebrity and domestic political news items with an entire issue devoted to his own front-line reporting of the <a href="/wiki/Cambodian_genocide" title="Cambodian genocide">genocide in Pol Pot\'s Cambodia</a>, not only outsold <i>The Sun</i> on the day it was issued but became the only edition of the <i>Daily Mirror</i> to ever sell every single copy issued throughout the country, something never achieved by <i>The Sun</i>.\n</p><p>In January 1986 Murdoch shut down the Bouverie Street premises of <i>The Sun</i> and <i>News of the World</i>, and moved operations to the new <a href="/wiki/Wapping" title="Wapping">Wapping</a> complex in East London, substituting the electricians\' union for the print unions as his production staff\'s representatives and greatly reducing the number of staff employed to print the papers; a year-long <a href="/wiki/Picketing_(protest)" class="mw-redirect" title="Picketing (protest)">picket</a> by sacked workers was eventually defeated (see <a href="/wiki/Wapping_dispute" title="Wapping dispute">Wapping dispute</a>).\n</p>\n<h4><span id=".22Freddie_Starr_Ate_My_Hamster.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="&quot;Freddie_Starr_Ate_My_Hamster&quot;">"Freddie Starr Ate My Hamster"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: &quot;Freddie Starr Ate My Hamster&quot;">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Freddiehamster.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b5/Freddiehamster.jpg/170px-Freddiehamster.jpg" width="170" height="214" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b5/Freddiehamster.jpg/255px-Freddiehamster.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/b/b5/Freddiehamster.jpg 2x" data-file-width="281" data-file-height="354" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Freddiehamster.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"Freddie Starr Ate My Hamster", 13 March 1986</div></div></div>\n<p>During this period, <i>The Sun</i> gained a reputation for running sensationalistic stories with questionable veracity. On 13 March 1986, the newspaper published one of its best known headlines: "FREDDIE STARR ATE MY HAMSTER".\n</p><p>The story alleged that British comedian <a href="/wiki/Freddie_Starr" title="Freddie Starr">Freddie Starr</a>, while staying at the home of a writer and old friend of his named Vince McCaffrey and his partner Lea LaSalle<sup id="cite_ref-Clifford_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clifford-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> in <a href="/wiki/Birchwood" title="Birchwood">Birchwood</a>, Cheshire, had, after returning from a performance at a nightclub in the early hours, found little to eat in their house. Starr put LaSalle\'s pet hamster, she was reported as saying, "between two slices of bread and started eating it".<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to <i>Max Clifford: Read All About It</i>, written by Clifford and Angela Levin, La Salle invented the story out of frustration with Starr who had been working on a book with McCaffrey. She contacted an acquaintance who worked for <i>The Sun</i> in Manchester. The story reportedly delighted MacKenzie, who was keen to run it, and Max Clifford, who had been Starr\'s public relations agent.<sup id="cite_ref-Clifford_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clifford-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Starr had to be persuaded that the apparent revelation would not damage him; the attention helped to revive his career.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> In his 2001 autobiography <i>Unwrapped</i>, Starr wrote that the incident was a complete fabrication: "I have never eaten or even nibbled a live hamster, gerbil, guinea pig, mouse, shrew, vole or any other small mammal."<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Elton_John_and_other_celebrities">Elton John and other celebrities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Elton John and other celebrities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Fuelled by MacKenzie\'s preoccupation with the subject, stories in <i>The Sun</i> insinuated and spread rumours about the sexual orientation of famous people, especially pop stars.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Eventually resulting in 17 libel writs in total, <i>The Sun</i> ran a series of false stories about the pop musician <a href="/wiki/Elton_John" title="Elton John">Elton John</a> from 25 February 1987.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> They began with an invented account of the singer having sexual relationships with rent boys. The singer-songwriter was abroad on the day indicated in the story, as former <i>Sun</i> journalist <a href="/wiki/John_Blake_(journalist)" title="John Blake (journalist)">John Blake</a>, recently poached by the <i>Daily Mirror</i>, soon discovered.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> After further stories, in September 1987, <i>The Sun</i> accused John of having his <a href="/wiki/Rottweiler" title="Rottweiler">Rottweiler</a> guard dogs\' <a href="/wiki/Surgical_debarking" class="mw-redirect" title="Surgical debarking">voice boxes surgically removed</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Chippindale322_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chippindale322-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> In November, the <i>Daily Mirror</i> found their rival\'s only source for the rent boy story and he admitted it was a totally fictitious concoction created for money.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> The inaccurate story about his dogs, actually <a href="/wiki/German_Shepherd" title="German Shepherd">Alsatians</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Chippindale322_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chippindale322-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> put pressure on <i>The Sun</i>, and John received \xc2\xa31&#160;million in an out of court settlement, then the largest damages payment in British history. <i>The Sun</i> ran a front-page apology on 12 December 1988, under the banner headline "SORRY, ELTON".<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> In May 1987, gay men were offered free one-way airline tickets to Norway to leave Britain for good: "Fly Away Gays \xe2\x80\x93 And We Will Pay" was the paper\'s headline.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Gay Church of England clergymen were described in one headline in November 1987 as "Pulpit poofs".<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Television personality <a href="/wiki/Piers_Morgan" title="Piers Morgan">Piers Morgan</a>, a former editor of the <i>Daily Mirror</i> and of <i>The Sun</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s "Bizarre" pop column, has said that during the late 1980s, at Kelvin MacKenzie\'s behest, he was ordered to speculate on the sexuality of male pop stars for a feature headlined "The Poofs of Pop".<sup id="cite_ref-Morgan05_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morgan05-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> He also recalls MacKenzie headlining a January 1989 story about the first same-sex kiss on the BBC television soap opera <i><a href="/wiki/EastEnders" title="EastEnders">EastEnders</a></i> "EastBenders",<sup id="cite_ref-Morgan05_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morgan05-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> describing the kiss between <a href="/wiki/Colin_Russell_(EastEnders)" title="Colin Russell (EastEnders)">Colin Russell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Guido_Smith" class="mw-redirect" title="Guido Smith">Guido Smith</a> as "a homosexual love scene between yuppie poofs ... when millions of children were watching".<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1990, the Press Council adjudicated against <i>The Sun</i> and columnist <a href="/wiki/Garry_Bushell" title="Garry Bushell">Garry Bushell</a> for their use of derogatory terminology about gays.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="AIDS">AIDS</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: AIDS">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p><i>The Sun</i> responded to the health crisis on 8 May 1983 with the headline: "US Gay Blood Plague Kills Three in Britain".<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 17 November 1989, <i>The Sun</i> headlined a page 2 news story titled "STRAIGHT SEX CANNOT GIVE YOU AIDS \xe2\x80\x93 OFFICIAL."<sup id="cite_ref-truth_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-truth-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> <i>The Sun</i> favourably cited the opinions of <a href="/wiki/Baron_Kilbracken" title="Baron Kilbracken">Lord Kilbracken</a>, a member of the <a href="/wiki/All-Party_Parliamentary_Group" class="mw-redirect" title="All-Party Parliamentary Group">All Parliamentary Group on AIDS</a>. Lord Kilbracken said that only one person out of the 2,372 individuals with <a href="/wiki/HIV/AIDS" title="HIV/AIDS">HIV/AIDS</a> mentioned in a specific <a href="/wiki/Department_of_Health_(United_Kingdom)" class="mw-redirect" title="Department of Health (United Kingdom)">Department of Health</a> report was not a member of a "high risk group", such as homosexuals and recreational drug users. <i>The Sun</i> also ran an editorial further arguing that "At last the truth can be told ... the risk of catching AIDS if you are heterosexual is \'statistically invisible\'. In other words, impossible. So now we know \xe2\x80\x93 everything else is homosexual propaganda." Although many other British press services covered Lord Kilbracken\'s public comments, none of them made the argument that the <i>Sun</i> did in its editorial and none of them presented Lord Kilbracken\'s ideas without context or criticism.<sup id="cite_ref-truth_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-truth-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Critics stated that both <i>The Sun</i> and Lord Kilbracken <a href="/wiki/Cherry_picking_(fallacy)" class="mw-redirect" title="Cherry picking (fallacy)">cherry-picked</a> the results from one specific study while ignoring other data reports on HIV infection and not just AIDS infection, which the critics viewed as unethical politicisation of a medical issue. Lord Kilbracken himself criticised <i>The Sun</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;s</span> editorial and the headline of its news story; he stated that while he thought that gay people were more at risk of developing AIDS it was still wrong to imply that no one else could catch the disease. The <a href="/wiki/Press_Council_(UK)" title="Press Council (UK)">Press Council</a> condemned <i>The Sun</i> for committing what it called a "gross distortion". <i>The Sun</i> later published an apology, which they ran on Page 28. Journalist <a href="/wiki/David_Randall" title="David Randall">David Randall</a> argued in the textbook <i>The Universal Journalist</i> that <i>The Sun</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;s</span> story was one of the worst cases of journalistic malpractice in recent history, putting its own readers in harm\'s way.<sup id="cite_ref-truth_77-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-truth-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Hillsborough_disaster_and_its_aftermath">The Hillsborough disaster and its aftermath</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: The Hillsborough disaster and its aftermath">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Hillsborough_disaster#The_Sun" title="Hillsborough disaster">Hillsborough disaster \xc2\xa7&#160;The Sun</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hillsborough_disaster_Sun.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/67/Hillsborough_disaster_Sun.jpg/170px-Hillsborough_disaster_Sun.jpg" width="170" height="212" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/67/Hillsborough_disaster_Sun.jpg/255px-Hillsborough_disaster_Sun.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/6/67/Hillsborough_disaster_Sun.jpg 2x" data-file-width="282" data-file-height="352" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hillsborough_disaster_Sun.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The Sun</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;s</span> front page on 19 April 1989. The allegations were later proven to be entirely false, with the Sun later admitting their decision to publish the allegations was the "blackest day in this newspaper\'s history."<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Sun_Liverpool.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/The_Sun_Liverpool.jpg/170px-The_Sun_Liverpool.jpg" width="170" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/The_Sun_Liverpool.jpg/255px-The_Sun_Liverpool.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d9/The_Sun_Liverpool.jpg 2x" data-file-width="311" data-file-height="466" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Sun_Liverpool.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Poster urging the Liverpool public not to purchase <i>The Sun</i>.</div></div></div>\n<p>At the end of the decade, <i>The Sun</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s coverage of the Hillsborough football stadium disaster in <a href="/wiki/Sheffield" title="Sheffield">Sheffield</a> on 15 April 1989, in which 96 people died as a result of their injuries, proved to be, as the paper later admitted, the "most terrible" blunder in its history.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Under a front-page headline "The Truth", the paper printed allegations provided to them that some fans picked the pockets of crushed victims, that others urinated on members of the emergency services as they tried to help and that some even assaulted a police constable "whilst he was administering the kiss of life to a patient."<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> Despite the headline, written by <a href="/wiki/Kelvin_MacKenzie" title="Kelvin MacKenzie">Kelvin MacKenzie</a>, the story was based on allegations either by unnamed and unattributable sources, or hearsay accounts of what named individuals had said \xe2\x80\x93 a fact made clear to MacKenzie by Harry Arnold, the reporter who wrote the story.<sup id="cite_ref-brook2005_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brook2005-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The front page caused outrage in <a href="/wiki/Liverpool" title="Liverpool">Liverpool</a>, where the paper lost more than three-quarters of its estimated 55,000 daily sales and still sells poorly in the city more than 25 years later (around 12,000).<sup id="cite_ref-brook2005_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brook2005-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> It is unavailable in parts of the city, as many newsagents refuse to stock it.<sup id="cite_ref-SunLiverpool_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SunLiverpool-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Publican_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Publican-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> It was revealed in a documentary called <i>Alexei Sayle\'s Liverpool</i>, aired in September 2008, that many Liverpudlians will not even take the newspaper for free, and those who do may simply burn or tear it up.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Local people often refer to the newspaper as "The Scum", with campaigners believing it handicapped their fight for justice.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Sun has lost many millions of pounds in revenue in sales and advertising from the boycott on Merseyside.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_repercussions">Later repercussions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Later repercussions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<p>On 7 July 2004, in response to verbal attacks in Liverpool on <a href="/wiki/Wayne_Rooney" title="Wayne Rooney">Wayne Rooney</a>, just before his transfer from <a href="/wiki/Everton_F.C." title="Everton F.C.">Everton</a> to <a href="/wiki/Manchester_United_F.C." title="Manchester United F.C.">Manchester United</a>, who had sold his life story to <i>The Sun</i>, the paper devoted a full-page editorial to an apology for the "awful error" of its Hillsborough coverage and argued that Rooney (who was only three years old at the time of Hillsborough) should not be punished for its "past sins". In January 2005, <i>The Sun</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;s</span> managing editor <a href="/wiki/Graham_Dudman" title="Graham Dudman">Graham Dudman</a> admitting the Hillsborough coverage was "the worst mistake in our history", added: "What we did was a terrible mistake. It was a terrible, insensitive, horrible article, with a dreadful headline; but what we\'d also say is: we have apologised for it, and the entire senior team here now is completely different from the team that put the paper out in 1989."<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In May 2006, Kelvin MacKenzie, <i>Sun</i> editor at the time of the Hillsborough disaster, returned to the paper as a columnist. Furthermore, on 11 January 2007, MacKenzie stated, while a panellist on BBC1\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Question_Time_(TV_series)" class="mw-redirect" title="Question Time (TV series)">Question Time</a></i>, that the apology he made about the coverage was a hollow one, forced upon him by <a href="/wiki/Rupert_Murdoch" title="Rupert Murdoch">Rupert Murdoch</a>. MacKenzie further claimed he was not sorry "for telling the truth" but he admitted that he did not know whether some Liverpool fans urinated on the police, or robbed victims.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 12 September 2012, following the publication of the official report into the disaster using previously withheld Government papers which officially <a href="/wiki/Exonerated" class="mw-redirect" title="Exonerated">exonerated</a> the Liverpool fans present, MacKenzie issued the following statement:\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Today I offer my profuse apologies to the people of Liverpool for that headline. I too was totally misled. Twenty three years ago I was handed a piece of copy from a reputable news agency in Sheffield [White\'s] in which a senior police officer and a senior local MP [Sheffield Hallam MP <a href="/wiki/Irvine_Patnick" title="Irvine Patnick">Irvine Patnick</a>] were making serious allegations against fans in the stadium. I had absolutely no reason to believe that these authority figures would lie and deceive over such a disaster. As the Prime Minister has made clear these allegations were wholly untrue and were part of a concerted plot by police officers to discredit the supporters thereby shifting the blame for the tragedy from themselves. It has taken more than two decades, 400,000 documents and a two-year inquiry to discover to my horror that it would have been far more accurate had I written the headline "The Lies" rather than "The Truth". I published in good faith and I am sorry that it was so wrong.\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>Trevor Hicks, chairman of the Hillsborough Family Support Group, rejected Mr MacKenzie\'s apology as "too little, too late", calling him "<a href="/wiki/Lowlife" class="mw-redirect" title="Lowlife">lowlife</a>, clever lowlife, but lowlife".<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Following the publication of the report <i>The Sun</i> apologised on its front page, under the headline "The Real Truth". With the newspaper\'s editor at the time, Dominic Mohan, adding underneath:\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p> It\'s a version of events that 23 years ago <i>The Sun</i> went along with and for that we\'re deeply ashamed and profoundly sorry. We\'ve co-operated fully with The Hillsborough Independent Panel and will publish reports of their findings in tomorrow\'s newspaper. We will also reflect our deep sense of shame".<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>Liverpool FC supporters and a significant majority of the City of Liverpool\'s residents have continued to boycott the newspaper as a result of the Hillsborough tragedy.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> In February 2017, Liverpool FC blocked the access of <i>Sun</i> journalists to its grounds, banning them from on-site coverage of matches and direct participation in press conferences. The newspaper said the decision "is bad for fans and bad for football".<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The newspaper was banned by <a href="/wiki/Everton_F.C." title="Everton F.C.">Everton F.C.</a> in April after <i>The Sun</i> published a column by former editor Kelvin MacKenzie the day before the 28th anniversary of the disaster which included a passage about footballer <a href="/wiki/Ross_Barkley" title="Ross Barkley">Ross Barkley</a> that was considered "appalling and indefensible" and included a racist epithet and insults against the people of Liverpool.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Access to the club grounds and facilities for <i>Sun</i> reporters were blocked. The <a href="/wiki/Mayor_of_Liverpool" title="Mayor of Liverpool">Mayor of Liverpool</a> <a href="/wiki/Joe_Anderson_(politician)" title="Joe Anderson (politician)">Joe Anderson</a> described the article as "disgrace" and a "slur" on the city.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> MacKenzie was suspended as a contributor to the paper on the day of publication.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_1990s">The 1990s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: The 1990s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:It%27s_The_Sun_Wot_Won_It.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/33/It%27s_The_Sun_Wot_Won_It.jpg/220px-It%27s_The_Sun_Wot_Won_It.jpg" width="220" height="287" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/3/33/It%27s_The_Sun_Wot_Won_It.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="276" data-file-height="360" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:It%27s_The_Sun_Wot_Won_It.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"<a href="/wiki/It%27s_The_Sun_Wot_Won_It" title="It&#39;s The Sun Wot Won It">It\'s The Sun Wot Won It</a>": front page of <i>The Sun</i> on 11 April 1992, after the Conservatives won <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1992" title="United Kingdom general election, 1992">the election</a>. The headline is regularly mentioned in debates about media influence in British politics.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p><i>The Sun</i> remained loyal to Thatcher right up to her resignation in November 1990,<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> despite the party\'s fall in popularity over the previous year following the introduction of the <a href="/wiki/Poll_tax_(Great_Britain)" title="Poll tax (Great Britain)">poll tax</a> (officially known as the Community Charge). This change to the way local government is funded was vociferously supported by the newspaper, despite widespread opposition, (some from Conservative MPs), which is seen as having contributed to Thatcher\'s own downfall. The tax was quickly repealed by her successor <a href="/wiki/John_Major" title="John Major">John Major</a>, whom <i>The Sun</i> initially supported enthusiastically,<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> believing the former <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer" title="Chancellor of the Exchequer">Chancellor of the Exchequer</a> was a radical Thatcherite.\n</p><p>On the day of the general election of 9 April 1992, its front-page headline, encapsulating its antipathy towards the Labour leader <a href="/wiki/Neil_Kinnock" title="Neil Kinnock">Neil Kinnock</a>, read "If Kinnock wins today, will the last person to leave Britain please turn out the lights". Two days later <i>The Sun</i> was so convinced its front page had swung a close election for the Conservatives it declared "<a href="/wiki/It%27s_The_Sun_Wot_Won_It" title="It&#39;s The Sun Wot Won It">It\'s The Sun Wot Won It</a>".\n</p><p><i>The Sun</i> led with a headline "Now we\'ve all been screwed by the cabinet" with a reference to <a href="/wiki/Black_Wednesday" title="Black Wednesday">Black Wednesday</a> on 17 September 1992, and the exposure a few months earlier of an extra-marital affair in which Cabinet Minister <a href="/wiki/David_Mellor" title="David Mellor">David Mellor</a> was involved.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> A month later, on 14 October, it attacked <a href="/wiki/Michael_Heseltine" title="Michael Heseltine">Michael Heseltine</a> for the mass coal <a href="/wiki/Mine_closure" title="Mine closure">mine closures</a>.\n</p><p>Despite its initial opposition to the closures, until 1997, the newspaper repeatedly called for the implementation of further Thatcherite policies, such as <a href="/wiki/Royal_Mail" title="Royal Mail">Royal Mail</a> privatisation,<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag needs to be fact-checked with the cited source(s). (June 2011)">verification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> and social security cutbacks, with leaders such as "Peter Lilley is right, we can\'t carry on like this".<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag needs to be fact-checked with the cited source(s). (June 2011)">verification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The paper showed hostility to the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> (EU) and approval of public spending cuts, tax cuts, and promotion of right-wing ministers to the cabinet, with leaders such as "More of the Redwood, not Deadwood".<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>The Sun</i> attacked Labour leader <a href="/wiki/John_Smith_(Labour_Party_leader)" title="John Smith (Labour Party leader)">John Smith</a> in February 1994, for saying that more British troops should be sent to <a href="/wiki/Bosnia" class="mw-redirect" title="Bosnia">Bosnia</a>. <i>The Sun</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;s</span> comment was that "The only serious radicals in British politics these days are the likes of Redwood, Lilley and Portillo".<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag needs to be fact-checked with the cited source(s). (June 2011)">verification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> It also gradually expressed its bitter disillusionment with John Major as Prime Minister, with leaders such as "What fools we were to back John Major".<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Between 1994 and 1996, <i>The Sun</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;s</span> circulation peaked. Its highest average sale was in the week ending 16 July 1994, when the daily figure was 4,305,957. The highest ever one-day sale was on 18 November 1995 (4,889,118), although the cover price had been cut to 10p. The highest ever one-day sale at full price was on 30 March 1996 (4,783,359).<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 22 January 1997, <i>The Sun</i> accused the shadow chancellor <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Brown" title="Gordon Brown">Gordon Brown</a> of stealing the Conservatives\' ideas by declaring, "If all he is offering is Conservative financial restraint, why not vote for the real thing?"<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> and called the planned windfall tax, which was later imposed by the Labour government, "wrongheaded".<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> In February 1997 it told Sir <a href="/wiki/Edward_Heath" title="Edward Heath">Edward Heath</a> MP to stand down for supporting a national minimum wage.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Support_for_New_Labour">Support for New Labour</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Support for New Labour">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p><i>The Sun</i> switched support to the Labour party on 18 March 1997, six weeks before the General Election victory which saw the <a href="/wiki/New_Labour" title="New Labour">New Labour</a> leader <a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a> become Prime Minister with a large parliamentary majority, despite the paper having attacked Blair and New Labour up to a month earlier. Its front-page headline read THE SUN BACKS BLAIR and its front-page editorial made clear that while it still opposed some New Labour policies, such as the minimum wage and devolution, it believed Blair to be "the breath of fresh air this great country needs".<sup id="cite_ref-archive1997_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-archive1997-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> John Major\'s Conservatives, it said, were "tired, divided and rudderless".<sup id="cite_ref-archive1997_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-archive1997-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> Blair, who had radically altered his party\'s image and policies, noting the influence the paper could have over its readers\' political thinking, had courted it (and Murdoch) for some time by granting exclusive interviews and writing columns.\n</p><p>In exchange for Rupert Murdoch\'s support, Blair agreed not to join the <a href="/wiki/European_Exchange_Rate_Mechanism" title="European Exchange Rate Mechanism">European Exchange Rate Mechanism</a> \xe2\x80\x93 which John Major had <a href="/wiki/Black_Wednesday" title="Black Wednesday">withdrawn the country from</a> in September 1992 after barely two years.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> Cabinet Minister <a href="/wiki/Peter_Mandelson" title="Peter Mandelson">Peter Mandelson</a> was "outed" by <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Parris" title="Matthew Parris">Matthew Parris</a> (a former <i>Sun</i> columnist) on BBC TV\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Newsnight" title="Newsnight">Newsnight</a></i> in November 1998. Misjudging public response, <i>The Sun</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;s</span> editor David Yelland demanded to know in a front-page editorial whether Britain was governed by a "gay mafia" of a "closed world of men with a mutual self-interest". Three days later the paper apologised in another editorial which said <i>The Sun</i> would never again reveal a person\'s sexuality unless it could be defended on the grounds of "overwhelming public interest".\n</p><p>In 2003 the paper was accused of racism by the government over its criticisms of what it perceived as the "open door" policy on immigration. The attacks came from the Prime Minister\'s press spokesman <a href="/wiki/Alastair_Campbell" title="Alastair Campbell">Alastair Campbell</a> and the Home Secretary <a href="/wiki/David_Blunkett" title="David Blunkett">David Blunkett</a> (later a <i>Sun</i> columnist). The paper rebutted the claim, believing that it was not racist to suggest that a "tide" of unchecked illegal immigrants was increasing the risk of terrorist attacks and infectious diseases. It did not help its argument by publishing a front-page story on 4 July 2003, under the headline "Swan Bake", which claimed that asylum seekers were slaughtering and eating swans. It later proved to have no basis in fact. Subsequently, <i>The Sun</i> published a follow-up headlined "Now they\'re after our fish!". Following a <a href="/wiki/Press_Complaints_Commission" title="Press Complaints Commission">Press Complaints Commission</a> adjudication a "clarification" was eventually printed, on page 41.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> In 2005 <i>The Sun</i> published photographs of Prince Harry sporting a Nazi costume to a fancy dress party. The photographs caused outrage across the world and <a href="/wiki/Clarence_House" title="Clarence House">Clarence House</a> was forced to issue a statement in response apologising for any offence or embarrassment caused.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Despite being a persistent critic of some of the government\'s policies, the paper supported Labour in both subsequent elections the party won. For the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2005" title="United Kingdom general election, 2005">2005 general election</a>, <i>The Sun</i> backed Blair and Labour for a third consecutive election win and vowed to give him "one last chance" to fulfil his promises, despite berating him for several weaknesses including a failure to control immigration. However, it did speak of its hope that the Conservatives (led by <a href="/wiki/Michael_Howard" title="Michael Howard">Michael Howard</a>) would one day be fit for a return to government.<sup id="cite_ref-StillShines_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StillShines-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> This election (Blair had declared it would be his last as prime minister) resulted in Labour\'s third successive win but with a much reduced majority.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Editorial_and_production_issues_in_the_2000s">Editorial and production issues in the 2000s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Editorial and production issues in the 2000s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Potters_Road_shops,_Barnet_(cropped).JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Potters_Road_shops%2C_Barnet_%28cropped%29.JPG/220px-Potters_Road_shops%2C_Barnet_%28cropped%29.JPG" width="220" height="186" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Potters_Road_shops%2C_Barnet_%28cropped%29.JPG/330px-Potters_Road_shops%2C_Barnet_%28cropped%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Potters_Road_shops%2C_Barnet_%28cropped%29.JPG/440px-Potters_Road_shops%2C_Barnet_%28cropped%29.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2338" data-file-height="1980" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Potters_Road_shops,_Barnet_(cropped).JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Sun</i>-branded newsagent shop</div></div></div>\n<p>When <a href="/wiki/Rebekah_Brooks" title="Rebekah Brooks">Rebekah Wade</a> (now Brooks) became editor in 2003, it was thought Page 3 might be dropped. Wade had tried to persuade <a href="/wiki/David_Yelland_(journalist)" title="David Yelland (journalist)">David Yelland</a>, her immediate predecessors in the job, to scrap the feature, but a model who shared her first name was used on her first day in the post.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 22 September 2003 the newspaper appeared to misjudge the public mood surrounding mental health, as well as its affection for former world heavyweight champion boxer <a href="/wiki/Frank_Bruno" title="Frank Bruno">Frank Bruno</a>, who had been admitted to hospital, when the headline "Bonkers Bruno Locked Up" appeared on the front page of early editions. The adverse reaction, once the paper had hit the streets on the evening of 21 September, led to the headline being changed for the paper\'s second edition to the more sympathetic "Sad Bruno in Mental Home".<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>The Sun</i> has been openly antagonistic towards other European nations, particularly the French and Germans. During the 1980s and 1990s, the nationalities were routinely described in copy and headlines as "frogs", "krauts" or "hun". As the paper is opposed to the EU it has referred to foreign leaders who it deemed hostile to the UK in unflattering terms. Former President <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Chirac" title="Jacques Chirac">Jacques Chirac</a> of France, for instance, was branded "le Worm". An unflattering picture of German chancellor <a href="/wiki/Angela_Merkel" title="Angela Merkel">Angela Merkel</a>, taken from the rear, bore the headline "I\'m Big in the Bumdestag" (17 April 2006).\n</p><p>Although <i>The Sun</i> was outspoken against the racism directed at <a href="/wiki/Bollywood" title="Bollywood">Bollywood</a> actress <a href="/wiki/Shilpa_Shetty" title="Shilpa Shetty">Shilpa Shetty</a> on television reality show <i><a href="/wiki/Celebrity_Big_Brother_5_(UK)" title="Celebrity Big Brother 5 (UK)">Celebrity Big Brother</a></i> during 2007, the paper captioned a picture on its website, from a Bollywood-themed pop video by <a href="/wiki/Hilary_Duff" title="Hilary Duff">Hilary Duff</a>, "Hilary <a href="/wiki/Papadum" title="Papadum">PoppaDuff</a>",<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> a very similar insult to that directed at Shetty.\n</p><p>On 7 January 2009, <i>The Sun</i> ran an exclusive front-page story claiming that participants in a discussion on Ummah.com, a <a href="/wiki/British_Muslim" class="mw-redirect" title="British Muslim">British Muslim</a> <a href="/wiki/Internet_forum" title="Internet forum">internet forum</a>, had made a "hate hit list" of <a href="/wiki/British_Jews" title="British Jews">British Jews</a> to be targeted by extremists over the <a href="/wiki/Gaza_War_(2008%E2%80%9309)" title="Gaza War (2008\xe2\x80\x9309)">Gaza War</a>. It was claimed that "Those listed [on the forum] should treat it very seriously. Expect a hate campaign and intimidation by 20 or 30 thugs." The UK magazine <i><a href="/wiki/Private_Eye" title="Private Eye">Private Eye</a></i> claimed that <a href="/wiki/Glen_Jenvey" title="Glen Jenvey">Glen Jenvey</a>, a man quoted by <i>The Sun</i> as a terrorism expert, who had been posting to the forum under the pseudonym "Abuislam", was the only forum member promoting a hate campaign while other members promoted peaceful advocacy, such as writing "polite letters". The story has since been removed from <i>The Sun</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;s</span> website following complaints to the UK\'s <a href="/wiki/Press_Complaints_Commission" title="Press Complaints Commission">Press Complaints Commission</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 9 December 2010, <i>The Sun</i> published a front-page story claiming that terrorist group <a href="/wiki/Al-Qaeda" title="Al-Qaeda">Al-Qaeda</a> had threatened a terrorist attack on <a href="/wiki/Granada_Television" class="mw-redirect" title="Granada Television">Granada Television</a> in Manchester to disrupt the episode of the soap opera <i><a href="/wiki/Coronation_Street" title="Coronation Street">Coronation Street</a></i> to be transmitted <a href="/wiki/Coronation_Street:_50th_anniversary_live_episode" class="mw-redirect" title="Coronation Street: 50th anniversary live episode">live</a> that evening. The paper cited unnamed sources, claiming "cops are throwing a ring of steel around tonight\'s live episode of <i>Coronation Street</i> over fears it has been targeted by Al-Qaeda."<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> Later that morning, however, <a href="/wiki/Greater_Manchester_Police" title="Greater Manchester Police">Greater Manchester Police</a> categorically denied having "been made aware of any threat from Al-Qaeda or any other proscribed organisation."<sup id="cite_ref-deny_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deny-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> <i>The Sun</i> published a small correction on 28 December, admitting "that while cast and crew were subject to full body searches, there was no
